<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="6ff95c5e22f640188e7f1b851e3eca57">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '9869a112-663c-4a19-89f1-75b801f8ae1d'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                var session_id = 'E94261D5CDA3829175FE7FE541043892';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')

                s.src = url;

                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d233368959" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>P2-phase layered cathodes play a pivotal role in sodium-ion batteries due to their efficient Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation chemistry. However, limited by crystal disintegration and interfacial instability, bulk and interfacial failure plague their electrochemical performance. To address these challenges, a structural enhancement combined with surface modification is achieved through trace Y doping. Based on a synergistic combination of experimental results and density functional theory (DFT) calculations, the introduction of partial Y ions at the Na site (2d) acts as a stabilizing pillar, mitigating the electrostatic repulsions between adjacent TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs and thereby relieving internal structural stress. Furthermore, the presence of Y effectively optimizes the Ni 3d-O 2p hybridization, resulting in enhanced electronic conductivity and a notable rapid charging ability, with a capacity of 77.3&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 40&nbsp;C. Concurrently, the introduction of Y also induces the formation of perovskite nano-islands, which serve to minimize side reactions and modulate interfacial diffusion. As a result, the refined P2-Na<sub>0.65</sub> Y<sub>0.025</sub>[Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode material exhibits an exceptionally low volume variation (≈1.99%), an impressive capacity retention of 83.3% even at −40&nbsp;°C after1500 cycles at 1&nbsp;C.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202310756-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202310756-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The constantly increasing energy consumption of modern society has stimulated the demand for large-scale energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0001_R_d233368951e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0002_R_d233368951e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Mater. Today">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have achieved commercial success, their suitability for large-scale electrical energy storage systems is constrained by the limited abundance of lithium resources (20&nbsp;ppm) and their geographically disparate distribution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0003_R_d233368951e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Small">3</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0004_R_d233368951e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), benefitting from more abundant elemental availability, are emerging as promising alternatives to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0005_R_d233368951e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew Chem Int Ed Engl">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In the pursuit of next-generation SIBs, there arises a need for cathode materials that exhibit not only high capacities and high redox potentials but also exceptional structural stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0006_R_d233368951e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Mater. Today">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among potential cathode materials, robust layered oxides stand out due to their environmental friendliness, chemically scalable synthesis, and stable crystal structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0007_R_d233368951e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Stor. Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0008_R_d233368951e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, P2-type layered oxides with wide interlayer spacing are the ideal choice for developing wide-temperature-range and fast-charge batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0006_R_d233368951e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Mater. Today">6</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0009_R_d233368951e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the path to commercializing P2-type layered oxide is beset with challenges such as rapid capacity fading and inferior power energy stemming from crystal disintegration and interfacial instability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0010_R_d233368951e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0011_R_d233368951e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - InfoMat">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For crystal disintegration, phase transitions are the main failure mechanisms upon Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and reinsertion. At a high state of charge (&gt; 4.0&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), the decreased shielding of Na<sup>+</sup>, will drive the gliding of TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs, and trigger the structural transition between the P- to O-type stackings.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0012_R_d233368951e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew Chem Int Ed Engl">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The irreversible P2→O2 phase transition causes a drastic volume change of 23% and restricts the Na diffusivity for the larger diffusion energy barrier in the O-type stackings.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0013_R_d233368951e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nano Energy">13</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0014_R_d233368951e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Energy">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although the OP4/ Z phase is reversible, the moderate structure variation still affects the high-rate capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0015_R_d233368951e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At a lower state of discharge, (&lt; 2.5&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), Jahn-Teller distortion associated with Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox couple induces detrimental P2–P2ʹ phase transformation thus resulting in structural defects and amorphization upon cycling. The Na<sup>+</sup> mobility is also decreased because Mn<sup>3+</sup> (<i>t<sub>2g</sub>3</i>, <i>e<sub>g</sub>1</i>) owns a stronger electron-dipolar interaction than Mn<sup>4+</sup>(<i>t<sub>2g</sub>3</i>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0016_R_d233368951e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Eng. J.">16</a>-<a href="#smll202310756-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0018_R_d233368951e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Lett.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides these structural failure modes in the host structure, interfacial instability, a consequence of chemical reactions at the cathode-electrolyte interphase (CEI), remains a paramount concern.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0019_R_d233368951e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nat. Commun.">19</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0020_R_d233368951e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew Chem Int Ed Engl">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Oxidative decomposition of electrolytes can generate hydrogen fluoride (HF), which attacks TM ions, accounting for irreversible active loss from the cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0021_R_d233368951e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Energy Lett.">21</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0022_R_d233368951e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Energy">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The continuous accumulation of the passivation layer at CEI will obstruct ion and electron diffusion, giving rise to increased internal impedance and capacity loss after prolonged cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0023_R_d233368951e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0024_R_d233368951e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, CEI may inevitably crack and reform for the repeated volume change during cycling, which further accelerates electrolyte decomposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0025_R_d233368951e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0026_R_d233368951e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The low ionic and electron conductivity coating also decreases specific capacity. On the other side, operating at low (≤ 0&nbsp;°C) and/ or high (&gt; 25&nbsp;°C) temperatures usually degenerates the battery performance and leads to safety risks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0009_R_d233368951e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0027_R_d233368951e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Sci. China Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More importantly, many literatures demonstrate that the CEI, but not the electrolyte conductivity and viscosity, govern the electrochemical property at wide temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0028_R_d233368951e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0029_R_d233368951e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - A. Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, bulk structure and CEI must be stabilized concurrently to harvest desired electrochemical performances.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0030_R_d233368951e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nat. Commun.">30</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0031_R_d233368951e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nat. Commun.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Currently, doping metal ions (Al<sup>3+</sup>, Ti<sup>4+</sup>, Sb<sup>5+</sup>) mitigate phase transitions by the increased oxygen binding energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0032_R_d233368951e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a>-<a href="#smll202310756-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0036_R_d233368951e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Sci. (Weinh)">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Inert coating layers (CuO, NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) are utilized to suppress the parasitic reaction at the interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0008_R_d233368951e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0037_R_d233368951e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Eng. J.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, excessive doping of inactive metal ions inevitably may sacrifice the cathode's specific capacity. With these perspectives, it is challenging and highly desirable that the dopants with trace amounts can enter the crystal bulk to restrain irreversible lattice distortion and form ion- and electron-conducting layers at the interface to hinder parasitic reaction for the commercial development of SIBs, but have not been investigated specifically.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, to address the shortcomings outlined above, we report a dual-stabilization effect of Y dopant on both the evolution of bulk structure and the cathode's interphase with the electrolyte during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and reinsertion. We reveal, from Rietveld refinement, and density functional calculations, that partial Y ions are utilized as functional dopants to rivet in the edge-sharing sites of Na<sup>+</sup> in P2-Na<sub>0.65</sub>Y<sub>0.025</sub>[Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (label as NNMY2). This placement fosters a robust Y─O bond with adjacent O atoms, effectively eliminating phase transitions during cycling due to the reinforced TMO<sub>6</sub>-octahedra slabs. Interestingly, another partial Y atoms adhere to expose the surface of particles, forming a perovskite-enriched layer. This coating layer hinders the parasitic reaction while enhancing the ions and electron diffusion at the interface. Remarkably, the trace amounts of Y dopant achieve cycling durability (an impressive 83.3% capacity retention after 1500 cycles at −40&nbsp;°C), and ultrahigh rate capacity (77.3&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 40&nbsp;C).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202310756-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202310756-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202310756-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202310756-sec-0030-title">2.1 Crystal Structure</h3>
            
            <p>Yttrium-doped P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>Y<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.01, 0.025, labeled as NNM, NNMY1, NNMY2, respectively) was synthesized via a co-precipitation method followed by solid-state reaction, however, the pure P2 phase was only obtained from <i>x</i> = 0 and 0.01, proved by the XRD patterns in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202310756-fig-0001">1a</a>. The NNMY2 samples were also indexed to hexagonal structure (space group: <i>P63/mmc</i>) with little impurities of perovskite phase, which have been observed in previous literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0038_R_d233368951e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Energy Stor. Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Their exact composition was examined by the inductively coupled plasma (ICP) method, as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Noticeably, the coherent phase with perovskite structure may be in favor of electron/ion conductivity, alleviating lattice distortion, and achieving pseudo zero-strain cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0038_R_d233368951e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Energy Stor. Mater.">38</a>-<a href="#smll202310756-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0040_R_d233368951e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nat Mater">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The shift of the (002) peak of NNMY2 samples toward a relatively high angle indicated that lattice cell shrinkage along the <i>c</i>-axis according to the Bragg equation (Equation (<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>) in Supporting information). This phenomenon ensured a confidence that the reduced O<sup>2−</sup>–O<sup>2−</sup> electrostatic repulsion between two TMO<sub>2</sub> layers, might contribute to the entry of partial Y ion into Na slabs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0041_R_d233368951e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">41</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0042_R_d233368951e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To figure out Y-doped sites, structural refinement with the two-phase model was conducted by putting Y ion at the Na site and TM site, respectively, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0001">1b,c</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The specific crystallographic data, atom coordinates, presented in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), exhibited that lattice parameter <i>c</i> of NNM, NNMY1, NNMY2 (Na-site), and NNMY2 (TM-site) were 11.157, 11.156, 11.147, and 11.158, respectively. The contracted <i>c</i>-axis suggested that Y ions prefer to occupy 2b sites (Na sites) rather than 2a sites (TM sites) in line with XRD patterns. Besides, the refined structure further confirmed the presence of the introduced perovskite phase with a weight fraction of approximately 3.7 wt%. To further verify possible sites of Y ion, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were carried out to reveal the occupancy behavior of Y ion in the NNMY2 sample. The diagrams for the crystal structure of NNM, and NNMY2 with 4 x 4 × 1 supercell were built in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a–d</a> (Supporting Information). The Y formation energy (−6.65&nbsp;eV/f.u.) in the Na<sub>e</sub> site was lower than that of Na<sub>f</sub> site (−6.23&nbsp;eV/f.u.) and TM sites (Ni: −4.83&nbsp;eV/f.u., Mn: −1.96&nbsp;eV/f.u.) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202310756-fig-0002">2d,e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), which demonstrated that Y was more inclined to remain in Na<sub>e</sub> sites. Previously, various kinds of foreign dopants, that was Na substituents (such as K<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>, and In<sup>3+</sup>), or Li<sup>+</sup> substituents (such as Na<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>) in layered oxides have been reported to markedly stabilize the alkali-metal slabs, and thus restraining lattice displacement.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202310756-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0017_R_d233368951e730" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Commun.">17</a>, <a href="#smll202310756-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0042_R_d233368951e730" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a>-<a href="#smll202310756-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202310756-bib-0046_R_d233368951e730" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Energy Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0001">1f,g</a>, electron localization function (ELF) with NNMY2 revealed Y dopant induced a significant increase in electronic localization around the oxygen atoms adjacent to the dopant site. These findings revealed the presence of stronger bond energy between Y and O atoms than Ni─O, and Mn─O (Y─O: 715&nbsp;kJ mol <sup>−1</sup>, Ni─O: 392&nbsp;kJ mol <sup>−1</sup>, Mn─O: 402&nbsp;kJ mol <sup>−1</sup>). Y─O bonds were severed as “pillars” in the Na layer to inhibit the structural collapse along the <i>c</i> direction. Moreover, Y<sup>3+</sup> shielded the electrostatic repulsions between theTMO<sub>2</sub> slabs and altered electronic properties, thus maintaining structural stability, and superior ion / electronic conductivity.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202310756-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/606a4d25-0fe8-45a0-8817-6af81203af08/smll202310756-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/606a4d25-0fe8-45a0-8817-6af81203af08/smll202310756-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f295c5c7-0194-400a-bbe5-21dcb128258f/smll202310756-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/606a4d25-0fe8-45a0-8817-6af81203af08/smll202310756-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202310756-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b395c778-8f5c-49d9-925f-0e77f4cda889/smll202310756-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b395c778-8f5c-49d9-925f-0e77f4cda889/smll202310756-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3f25dd22-c5cd-4863-bddb-95f86423bfd0/smll202310756-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b395c778-8f5c-49d9-925f-0e77f4cda889/smll202310756-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202310756-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202310756" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202310756-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202310756-sec-0040-title">2.2 Morphological Structure</h3>
            
            <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images for NNM and NNMY2 samples were displayed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0002">2a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a> (Supporting Information). On the one hand, the NNMY2 sample displayed nano-islands and a particle size ranging between 3 and 5&nbsp;µm, while the surface of the NNM particle was clean and smooth. To confirm the Y ion distribution in primary particles, a random region was singled out to perform energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) elementary mapping for NNMY2. The Y ion showed a gradient distribution in a primary particle, with a small amount in the bulk structure. Other maximal content at the surface with nanoislands, which was in line with reported phenomena of element surface enrichment in the layered structure. And other metal elements (Ni, Mn, Na) were all uniform distribution. On the other hand, the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image of two surface regions was acquired (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0002">2b</a>), from which the d-spacing value of ≈4.9 Å was assigned to the (002) plane of the <i>P63/mmc</i> layered structure, and 2.2 Å for the (211) plane of perovskite phase, showing the intertwined region could be up to ≈5–8&nbsp;nm. Additionally, the larger lattice spacing (5.4 Å) of NNM compared to NNMY2 along the same (002) plane demonstrated that Y ions were riveted in the Na sites, which was consistent with XRD results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b–d</a>, Supporting Information). In brief, as illustrated in the schematic in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0002">2c</a>, a strategic design was optimized to concurrently achieve a perovskite surface coating and Y doping within the Na interlayer for layered cathodes. Y ion in the Na interlayer could serve as a “pillar” to shield the electrostatic repulsions between the TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs, bolstering bulk structural stability. Perovskite nano-islands with ion/ electron conductivity could modify interfacial reactions, inhibiting constant electrolyte corrosion, and even regulating the ion diffusion as Na<sup>+</sup> was inserted and extracted from the solid matrix.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202310756-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202310756-sec-0050-title">2.3 Na-Ion Storage Performance</h3>
            
            <p>Inspired by the essential role played by the Y dopant in the bulk structure and interphase, the electrochemical properties of NNM and NNMY2 were evaluated by half-cells operated within the voltage window of 2.4–4.15&nbsp;V. The galvanostatic charge–discharge voltage and the corresponding cyclic voltammetry (CV) profiles of NNM and NNMY2 exhibited a gradually shortened plateau beyond 4.0&nbsp;V after Y doping, which could stem from the restrained phase transition (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202310756-fig-0003">3a,b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information, 1&nbsp;C = 170&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, 25&nbsp;°C). Moreover, NNMY2 with a trace of Y showed no specific capacity decay (≈ 91.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) over 5 cycles at room temperature, whereas NNM experienced distinct capacity decay in the second cycle. To confirm the practical application of NNMY2 over a wide temperature, all-climate performance evaluations were carried out from −40 to 40&nbsp;°C, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0003">3c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). Interestingly, the profiles obtained at elevated temperatures presented a significant degree of overlap with the one at 25&nbsp;°C. Meanwhile, when it dropped to −40&nbsp;°C, the capacity loss was only 8.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> relative to room temperature, and there were fairly stable polarization and voltage platforms. The remarkable ability to perform consistently over a wide temperature suggests an extremely fast ion kinetics both at the interface and in the lattice. Furthermore, a stable interface film has been formed even charging/discharging process. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0003">3d</a> further displayed the rate performance of NNM and NNMY2 at different temperatures. NNM delivered discharge capacities of 82.5, 81.7, 80.4, 77.4, 73.4, and 55.3&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, and 20 C rates, respectively. After the Y substitution, rate properties were vastly improved from 40 to −40&nbsp;°C. Even at a 40&nbsp;C rate, NNMY2 still manifested a specific capacity of 77.3&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (corresponding to 84% capacity retention at 0.5&nbsp;C), and discharge capacity of 58.3&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at −40&nbsp;°C with a current density of 10&nbsp;C. With respect to cycling performance at 5C, NNMY2 could retain 80.3% of the initial capacity after 500 cycles, while NNM only possessed a 16.5% retention after 200 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0003">3e</a>). Furthermore, the cycling performance of NNMY2 at both 40 and −40&nbsp;°C was also surveyed, as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0003">3f</a>, where prominent capacity retention rates are 83.3% over 1500 cycles at −40&nbsp;°C, which meant superior ionic diffusion both in the lattice and at the interface, and 79.2% over 400 cycles at 40&nbsp;°C owing to the reduced interfacial side effects. The comparisons of electrochemical performance with other cathodes are listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), revealing that NNMY2 showed remarkable rate and cycle performance. Furthermore, we also investigated its practicability when the full cell based on NNMY2 cathode and presodiated hard carbon was assembled with configuration using the 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in a diglyme electrolyte solution (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0003">3g</a>). As displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), to match full battery more reasonably, full batteries were assembled with an n/p ratio of 1.2/1 and electrolyte amount of 50&nbsp;uL, according to the specific capacity and CV tests of two samples in a half-cell device. The ratio between electrolyte amount and cell capacity was reasonable for coin cell full-cells, even though it might be slightly high for practical large format cells. The full cell delivered a specific capacity of 75&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 2.3–4.14&nbsp;V at 0.5&nbsp;C and 25&nbsp;°C, corresponding to a specific energy of 243&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> based on the mass of cathode active material, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). Moreover, even tested at 10&nbsp;C, it still maintained a reversible capacity of 69.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Also, capacity retention was still as high as 74.0% after 500 cycles at 5&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0003">3h</a>), which demonstrated the practical application for large-scale EESs. These full-cell performances were well-positioned compared to the state-of-the-art literature on Na-ion layered cathodes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202310756-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202310756-sec-0060-title">2.4 Na-Ion Kinetics</h3>
            
            <p>To further elucidate the origin of excellent electrochemical performance, NNMY2 electrode kinetics were investigated at wide temperatures. As displayed in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#smll202310756-fig-0004">4a</a>, <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a,c</a> (Supporting Information), the combined diffusion-controlled and capacitive behavior originated from the cathodic and anodic peaks shifted to relatively low and high potential ranges, respectively. Na-ion diffusion behavior was accurately disentangled through Equation (<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>), Equation (<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>), and Equation (<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>) in the Supporting information. The <i>b</i> value was determined by plotting a log (<i>i</i>)-log (<i>v</i>) graph and listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). In our cases, as plotted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0004">4b</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b,d</a>, and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information), the calculated <i>b</i> values of the NNMY2 electrode at 25 or −40&nbsp;°C were &gt; 0.5, and high capacitive behavior ratios were exhibited at all the rates, indicating higher ratios of the kinetic-favorable capacitive behavior. Furthermore, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was employed to assess specific Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D<sub>Na+</sub></i>) according to Equation (<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>) of Supporting information (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0004">4c,d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). At the same test condition, it was promising that the calculated <i>D<sub>Na+</sub></i> value of the NNMY2 electrode did not fluctuate much from −40&nbsp;°C (10<sup>−10.37</sup>–10<sup>−9.25</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) to 40&nbsp;°C (10<sup>−10.7</sup>–10<sup>−9.25</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>), indicating superior kinetics. By contrast, pristine NNM exhibited <i>D<sub>Na+</sub></i> of 10<sup>−12.8</sup>–10<sup>−9.47</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> at 25&nbsp;°C, which decreased to approximately an order of magnitude that for NNMY2 confirming unstable structure. Besides, the nudged elastic band (NEB) method was also carried out to evaluate the Na<sup>+</sup> migration barrier. Based on the fact of Na<sup>+</sup> occupation and their surrounding environments, the designed diffusion path was not Na<sub>e1</sub> to Na<sub>e2</sub>, but Na<sub>e1</sub> to Na<sub>f</sub>, and then back to Na<sub>e2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0004">4e</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0004">4f</a>, NNMY2 encountered a small of 0.369&nbsp;eV compared to 0.436&nbsp;eV for NNM.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202310756-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7d3c034c-5fe7-4ecc-aacf-c5e10e473240/smll202310756-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7d3c034c-5fe7-4ecc-aacf-c5e10e473240/smll202310756-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/363930cd-79b4-4d15-a75c-fc0b9206b4dc/smll202310756-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7d3c034c-5fe7-4ecc-aacf-c5e10e473240/smll202310756-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202310756-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202310756-sec-0070-title">2.5 Structural Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>In situ, XRD was carried to obtain insights into the bulk-structural evolution of the NNMY2 sample during or after cycling 2.4–4.15&nbsp;V. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0004">4g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information), no new peaks appeared, the (002) and (004) peaks consecutively shifted to lower angle, while (100) and (102) diffraction lines moved to higher angle, indicating the increase of lattice parameter <i>c</i> and the decrease of lattice parameter <i>a</i> upon Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation. This behavior was related to an increase in O─O electrostatic repulsion between TM layers after the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>. After the full cycle, the shifted diffraction peaks reverted to their original position, which confirmed the structural robustness. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0004">4h</a> exhibited the low <i>a</i>-axis variation (≈1.21%) and volume change (≈1.99%), accounting for the durable electrochemical performance. In addition, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis was applied to further investigate the composition of the interface after 100 cycles, and the intensities of XPS spectra were normalized, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0004">4i–k</a>. The C 1s peaks disclosed the existence of C─O and C═O, owing to the electrolyte decomposition. Meanwhile, the peaks at ca 285&nbsp;eV were related to Super P (C─C), while the peaks located at 290&nbsp;eV represented PVDF binder (C─F). For the O 1s peaks. NNM samples exhibited an obviously increased peak assigned to C─O and C═O groups. Besides, the peak intensity of NaF in NNMY2 was lower than NNM, validating less parasitic reaction and stable CEI films. Therefore, such results have suggested that the interface stability had indeed been improved, which could be ascribed to the stable Y-enriched phase at the interface. On the side, perovskite nano-islands could restrict the direct contact between the cathode and electrolyte, forming more stable and thinner CEI films. On the other hand, a more stable structure inhibited the release of oxidizing species, thus mitigating electrolyte decomposition. Furthermore, EIS (electrochemical impedance of spectroscopy) results were fitted with an equivalent circuit to identify the high-frequency Ohmic resistance (<i>R</i><sub>0</sub>), solid electrolyte interphase resistance (<i>R</i><sub>1</sub>), and charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>2</sub>), as depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information) presented all the resistances of NNM and NNMY2 calculated from EIS data. The small variation in <i>R<sub>1</sub></i> values and low charge-transfer resistance of NNMY2 electrode possessed stable CEI films for the existence of perovskite nano-islands with ion/ electron conductivity, thus which contributed to mitigating the kinetic hindrance and improving the durability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202310756-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202310756-sec-0080-title">2.6 Simulated Atomic and Electronic Structures</h3>
            
            <p>The atomic and electronic structures of NNM and NNMY2 can be analyzed by DFT calculations. The crystal orbital Hamilton populations (COHP) in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202310756-fig-0005">5a–c</a> indicated that NNMY2 improved the electrostatic cohesion of the alkai-metal layer due to its stronger bond strength and smaller bond length for the larger integrated COHP of Y─O than Na─O. For atomistic details, the calculated density of states (DOS) was conducted in Na<sub>x</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>x</sub>Y<sub>0.025</sub>[Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> when Na was emigrated (<i>x</i> = 0.75, 0.67, 0.5, 0.375, 0.22). The total DOS plots suggested NNM compound possessed the semiconductor feature (bandgap = 0.64&nbsp;eV), whereas no bandgap was observed after Y doping, which exhibited higher electronic conductivity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the effect of Y doping became more significant after charging according to the partial density of states (pDOS), as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0005">5d</a>, and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). After the Y modification, the O 2p orbital came closer to the Fermi level and overlapped the Ni 3d orbital, which might provide significant electroconducting capability. This finding also implied that O<sup>2−</sup>/(O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>n−</sup> reaction occurs at a low charge voltage, which could donate extra charge during Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation. The ex-situ XPS analysis shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202310756-fig-0005">5f–j</a>, further confirmed this result. When charged to ≈3.2&nbsp;V, a new peak emerged at 531.2&nbsp;eV and was maintained upon Na removal at a high potential. The new peak could be attributed to the participation of oxygen in the redox reaction. Moreover, stronger Ni 3d–O 2p hybridization favored the formation of a stable Ni─O bonding state, promoting the reversibility of O<sup>2−</sup>/(O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>n−</sup> redox.</p>

         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202310756-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202310756-sec-0090-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the bulk/ interfacial reactions were synchronously regulated through trace Y doping. The partial Y ions were rivetted in the edge-sharing sites of Na<sup>+</sup> in P2- Na<sub>0.65</sub> Y<sub>0.025</sub>[Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, which formed a strong Y─O bond with adjacent O atoms, thus enhancing the electrostatic cohesion of alkali-metal layer. Moreover, the increased electronic conductivity and Ni 3d–O 2p hybridization resulted from the Y element substitution. Besides, the perovskite-enriched layer induced by Y substitution hindered the parasitic reaction, and promoted the ions and electron diffusion at the interface, thus stabilizing the Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation reaction during the cycling. The as-prepared NNMY2 electrode possessed a stable cycle lifespan with 80.3% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 5&nbsp;C and 25&nbsp;°C, and ultrahigh rate capacity delivering 77.3&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 40 C. Meanwhile, NNMY2 also exhibited remarkable electrochemical performance at wide temperatures (83.3% capacity retention after 1500 cycles at −40&nbsp;°C, 79.2% over 400 cycles at 40&nbsp;°C). This study can broaden the perspectives for achieving a highly stable crystal structure, and make progress toward SIB commercialization.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

    window.addEventListener('load', () => {
        document.querySelectorAll('.admin-bar-popup-menu ul li')?.forEach(function (li) {
            const anchor = li.querySelector('a');
            if (anchor && anchor.textContent.trim() === 'Edit Access Denial') {
                li.style.display = 'none';
